--- Chunk 410 ---
Originally released in 1908, it was the first affordable automobile and dominated sales for years.

--- Chunk 530 ---
<td class="sidebar-navbar">

--- Chunk 531 ---
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1129693374" /><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1239400231">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}@media(prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}}@media print{.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:none!important}}</style>

--- Chunk 532 ---

--- Chunk 533 ---
<li class="nv-view">

--- Chunk 534 ---
<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Template:Economy_of_the_United_States_sidebar" title="Template:Economy of the United States sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a>

--- Chunk 535 ---
<li class="nv-talk">

--- Chunk 536 ---
<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Template_talk:Economy_of_the_United_States_sidebar" title="Template talk:Economy of the United States sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a>

--- Chunk 537 ---
<li class="nv-edit">

--- Chunk 538 ---
<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:EditPage/Template:Economy_of_the_United_States_sidebar" title="Special:EditPage/Template:Economy of the United States sidebar"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a>

--- Chunk 539 ---
In the United States, the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Automotive_industry" title="Automotive industry">automotive industry</a> began in the 1890s and, as a result of the size of the domestic market and the use of mass production, rapidly evolved into the largest in the world.

--- Chunk 540 ---
The United States was the first country in the world to have a mass market for vehicle production and sales and is a pioneer of the automotive industry<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and mass market production process.

--- Chunk 541 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> During the 20th century, global competitors emerged, especially in the second half of the century primarily across European and Asian markets, such as Germany, France, Italy, Japan and South Korea.

--- Chunk 542 ---
S.  is currently second among the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_motor_vehicle_production" title="List of countries by motor vehicle production">largest manufacturers in the world by volume</a>.

--- Chunk 543 ---
By value, the U.

--- Chunk 544 ---
S.  was the world's largest importer and fourth-largest exporter of cars in 2023.

--- Chunk 545 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>

--- Chunk 546 ---
American manufacturers produce approximately 10 million units annually.

--- Chunk 547 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-statista.com_5-0" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-statista.com-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Notable exceptions were 5.7 million automobiles manufactured in 2009 (due to <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_the_2008%E2%80%9310_automotive_industry_crisis_on_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Effects of the 2008–10 automotive industry crisis on the United States">crisis</a>), and more recently 8.8 million units in 2020 due to the global COVID-19 pandemic.

--- Chunk 548 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-statista.com_5-1" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-statista.com-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
Production peaked during the 1970s and early 2000s at 13–15 million units.

--- Chunk 549 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-OICA_7-0" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-OICA-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-OICAmore_8-0" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-OICAmore-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WMVD_9-0" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-WMVD-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>

--- Chunk 550 ---
Starting with <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Duryea_Motor_Wagon_Company" title="Duryea Motor Wagon Company">Duryea</a> in 1895, at least 1,900 different companies have been formed, producing over 3,000 makes of American automobiles.

--- Chunk 551 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> World War I (1917–1918) and the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Depression_in_the_United_States" title="Great Depression in the United States">Great Depression in the United States</a> (1929–1939) combined to drastically reduce the number of both major and minor producers.

--- Chunk 552 ---
During World War II, all the auto companies switched to making military equipment and weapons.

--- Chunk 553 ---
By the end of the 1950s the remaining smaller producers disappeared or merged into amalgamated corporations.

--- Chunk 554 ---
The industry was dominated by three large companies: <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company" title="Ford Motor Company">Ford</a>, and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chrysler" title="Chrysler">Chrysler</a>, all based in <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metro_Detroit" title="Metro Detroit">Metro Detroit</a>.

--- Chunk 555 ---
Those "<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_Three_(automobile_manufacturers)" title="Big Three (automobile manufacturers)">Big Three</a>" continued to prosper, and the U.

--- Chunk 556 ---
S.  produced three-quarters of all automobiles in the world by 1950, 8.0 million out of 10.6 million produced.

--- Chunk 557 ---
In 1908, 1 percent of U.

--- Chunk 558 ---
S.  households owned at least one automobile, while 50 percent did in 1948 and 75 percent did in 1960.

--- Chunk 559 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-Putnam_p._217_11-0" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-Putnam_p._217-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Imports from abroad were a minor factor before the 1960s.

--- Chunk 560 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-OICAmore_8-1" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-OICAmore-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WMVD_9-1" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-WMVD-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>

--- Chunk 561 ---
Beginning in the 1970s, a combination of high oil prices and increased competition from foreign auto manufacturers severely affected the US companies.

--- Chunk 562 ---
In the ensuing years, the US companies periodically bounced back, but by 2008 the industry was in turmoil due to the aforementioned <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_the_2008%E2%80%9310_automotive_industry_crisis_on_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Effects of the 2008–10 automotive industry crisis on the United States">crisis</a>.

--- Chunk 563 ---
As a result, General Motors and Chrysler filed for bankruptcy reorganization and were bailed out with loans and investments from the federal government.

--- Chunk 564 ---
June 2014 seasonally adjusted annualized sales were the biggest in history, with 16.98 million vehicles and toppled the previous record of July 2006.

--- Chunk 565 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Chrysler later merged into Fiat as <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fiat_Chrysler_Automobiles" title="Fiat Chrysler Automobiles">Fiat Chrysler</a> and is today a part of the multinational <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stellantis" title="Stellantis">Stellantis</a> group.

--- Chunk 566 ---
American electric automaker <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tesla,_Inc" class="mw-redirect" title="Tesla, Inc">Tesla</a> emerged onto the scene in 2009 and has since grown to be one of the world's <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Market_capitalization" title="Market capitalization">most valuable</a> companies, producing around 1/4th of the world's fully-electric passenger cars.

--- Chunk 567 ---
Prior to the 1980s, most manufacturing facilities were owned by the Big Three (GM, Ford, Chrysler) and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Motors_Corporation" title="American Motors Corporation">AMC</a>.

--- Chunk 568 ---
S.  market share has dropped steadily as numerous foreign-owned car companies have built factories in the U.

--- Chunk 569 ---
As of 2012, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Toyota" title="Toyota">Toyota</a> had 31,000 U.

--- Chunk 570 ---
S.  employees, compared to Ford's 80,000 and Chrysler's 71,100.

<sup id="cite_ref-money-go_14-0" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-money-go-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>

--- Chunk 576 ---
<h2 id="History">

--- Chunk 577 ---
<span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>

--- Chunk 578 ---
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" />

--- Chunk 579 ---
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">

--- Chunk 580 ---
See also: <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_automobile" title="History of the automobile">History of the automobile</a>

--- Chunk 581 ---
The <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oldsmobile_Curved_Dash" title="Oldsmobile Curved Dash">Oldsmobile Curved Dash</a> and the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ford_Model_T" title="Ford Model T">Ford Model T</a>, both American cars, are considered the first mass-produced<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and mass-affordable<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> cars, respectively.

--- Chunk 582 ---
American automotive company <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors</a> held the title of the world's best-selling automaker from 1931 to 2008.

--- Chunk 583 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The United States was the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_motor_vehicle_production" title="List of countries by motor vehicle production">second-largest manufacturer of motor vehicles</a> in 2023<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and is home to <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tesla,_Inc." title="Tesla, Inc.">Tesla</a>, the world's most valuable car company.

--- Chunk 584 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The United States is the world's second-largest automobile market by sales, having been overtaken by China in 2010.

--- Chunk 585 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>

--- Chunk 588 ---
<span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Production"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>

--- Chunk 591 ---
Trucks' share of US vehicles produced, has tripled since 1975.

--- Chunk 592 ---
Though vehicle fuel efficiency has increased within each category, the overall trend toward less efficient types of vehicles has offset some of the benefits of greater fuel economy and reduction of pollution and carbon dioxide emissions.

--- Chunk 593 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-EPA_AutomotiveTrends_202212_21-0" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-EPA_AutomotiveTrends_202212-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Without the shift towards SUVs, energy use per unit distance could have fallen 30% more than it did from 2010 to 2022.

--- Chunk 594 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-GlobalFuelEfficInit_202311_22-0" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-GlobalFuelEfficInit_202311-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>

--- Chunk 595 ---
The development of self-powered vehicles was accompanied by numerous technologies and components giving rise to numerous supplier firms and associated industries.

--- Chunk 596 ---
Various types of energy sources were employed by early automobiles including steam, electric, and gasoline.

--- Chunk 597 ---
Thousands of entrepreneurs were involved in developing, assembling, and marketing of early automobiles on a small and local scale.

--- Chunk 598 ---
Increasing sales facilitated production on a larger scale in factories with broader market distribution.

--- Chunk 599 ---
<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ransom_E._Olds" title="Ransom E. Olds">Ransom E. Olds</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thomas_B._Jeffery_Company" title="Thomas B. Jeffery Company">Thomas B. Jeffery</a> began mass production of their automobiles.

--- Chunk 600 ---
<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Henry_Ford" title="Henry Ford">Henry Ford</a> focused on producing an automobile that many middle class Americans could afford.

--- Chunk 601 ---
A patent filed by <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_B._Selden" title="George B. Selden">George B. Selden</a> on 8 May 1879 covered not only his engine but its use in a four-wheeled car.

--- Chunk 602 ---
Selden filed a series of amendments to his application which stretched out the legal process, resulting in a delay of 16 years before the patent was granted on 5 November 1895.

--- Chunk 603 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Selden licensed his patent to most major American automakers, collecting a fee on each car they produced and creating the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Association_of_Licensed_Automobile_Manufacturers" title="Association of Licensed Automobile Manufacturers">Association of Licensed Automobile Manufacturers</a>.

--- Chunk 604 ---
The Ford Motor Company fought this patent in court, and eventually won on appeal.

--- Chunk 605 ---
Henry Ford testified that the patent did more to hinder than encourage development of autos in the United States.

--- Chunk 606 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>

--- Chunk 607 ---
Originally purchased by wealthy individuals, by 1916 cars<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (August 2021)">which?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> began selling at $875.

--- Chunk 608 ---
Soon, the market widened with the mechanical betterment of the cars, the reduction in prices, as well as the introduction of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Installment_sale" title="Installment sale">installment sales</a> and payment plans.

--- Chunk 609 ---
During the period from 1917 to 1926, the annual rate of increase in sales was considerably less than from 1903 to 1916.

--- Chunk 610 ---
In the years 1918, 1919, 1921, and 1924 there were absolute declines in automotive production.

--- Chunk 611 ---
The automotive industry caused a massive shift in the industrial revolution because it accelerated growth by a rate never before seen in the U.

--- Chunk 612 ---
The combined efforts of innovation and industrialization allowed the automotive industry to take off during this period and it proved to be the backbone of United States manufacturing during the 20th century.

State involvement

--- Chunk 632 ---

--- Chunk 635 ---

--- Chunk 637 ---

--- Chunk 638 ---

<sup id="cite_ref-woutat19850106_28-0" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-woutat19850106-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the early 1900s, the U.

--- Chunk 674 ---
S.  saw the rise of the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_Three_(automobile_manufacturers)#United_States" title="Big Three (automobile manufacturers)">Big Three automakers</a>, Ford, GM, and Chrysler.

--- Chunk 675 ---
The industry became centered around Detroit, in Michigan, and adjacent states and in nearby Ontario, Canada.

--- Chunk 676 ---
Historian John Rae summarizes the explanations provided by historians: a central geographic location, water access, and an established industrial base with many skilled engineers.

--- Chunk 677 ---
The key factor was that Detroit was the base for highly talented entrepreneurs who saw the potential of the automobile: Henry Ford, Ransom E.

--- Chunk 678 ---
Chapin, Henry Joy, William C.

--- Chunk 679 ---
Durant, Howard E.

--- Chunk 680 ---
Coffin, John Dodge and Horace Dodge, and Benjamin Briscoe and Frank Briscoe.

--- Chunk 681 ---
From 1900 to 1915 these men transformed the fledgling industry into an international business.

--- Chunk 682 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>

--- Chunk 683 ---
<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Henry_Ford" title="Henry Ford">Henry Ford</a> began building cars in 1896 and started his own company in 1903.

--- Chunk 684 ---
The <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company" title="Ford Motor Company">Ford Motor Company</a> improved <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mass-production" class="mw-redirect" title="Mass-production">mass-production</a> with the first conveyor belt-based assembly line in 1913, producing the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Model_T" class="mw-redirect" title="Model T">Model T</a>, which had been introduced in 1908.

--- Chunk 685 ---
These assembly lines significantly reduced costs.

--- Chunk 686 ---
The first models were priced at $850, but by 1924 had dropped to $290.

--- Chunk 687 ---
The Model T sold extremely well and Ford became the largest automobile company in the U.

--- Chunk 688 ---
By the time it was retired in 1927, more than 15 million Model Ts had been sold.

--- Chunk 689 ---
Ford introduced the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ford_Model_A_(1927%E2%80%9331)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ford Model A (1927–31)">Model A</a> in 1927, after a six-month production stoppage to convert from the Model T, and produced it to 1931.

--- Chunk 690 ---
While the Model A was successful, Ford lost ground to GM and eventually Chrysler, as auto buyers looked to more upscale cars and newer styling.

--- Chunk 691 ---
Ford was a pioneer in establishing foreign manufacturing facilities, with production facilities created in England in 1911, and Germany and Australia in 1925.

--- Chunk 692 ---
Ford purchased the luxury <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lincoln_Motor_Company" title="Lincoln Motor Company">Lincoln</a> automaker in 1922 and established the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mercury_(automobile)" title="Mercury (automobile)">Mercury</a> division in 1939.

--- Chunk 693 ---
<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors Corporation</a> (GM), the company that soon became the world's largest automaker, was founded in 1908 by <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_C._Durant" title="William C. Durant">William Durant</a>.

--- Chunk 694 ---
Durant had previously been a carriage maker, and had taken control of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Buick" title="Buick">Buick</a> in 1904.

--- Chunk 695 ---
In 1908, the company initially acquired <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Buick" title="Buick">Buick</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oldsmobile" title="Oldsmobile">Oldsmobile</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oakland_Motor_Car_Company" title="Oakland Motor Car Company">Oakland</a> (later to become <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pontiac_(automobile)" title="Pontiac (automobile)">Pontiac</a>).

--- Chunk 696 ---
In 1909, GM acquired <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cadillac" title="Cadillac">Cadillac</a>, along with a number of other car companies and parts suppliers.

--- Chunk 697 ---
Durant was interested in acquiring Ford, but after initial merger talks, Henry Ford decided to keep his company independent.

--- Chunk 698 ---
In 1910, Durant lost control of GM after over-extending the company with its acquisitions.

--- Chunk 699 ---
A group of banks took over control of GM and ousted Durant.

--- Chunk 700 ---
Durant and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Louis_Chevrolet" title="Louis Chevrolet">Louis Chevrolet</a> founded <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chevrolet" title="Chevrolet">Chevrolet</a> in 1913 and it quickly became very successful.

--- Chunk 701 ---
Durant began acquiring stock in GM and by 1915 had majority control.

--- Chunk 702 ---
Chevrolet was acquired by GM in 1917 and Durant was back in charge of GM.

--- Chunk 703 ---
In 1921, Durant was again forced out of the company.

--- Chunk 704 ---
During the late 1920s, General Motors overtook Ford to become the largest automaker.

--- Chunk 705 ---
Under the leadership of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alfred_P._Sloan" title="Alfred P. Sloan">Alfred P. Sloan</a>, General Motors instituted <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Decentralized" class="mw-redirect" title="Decentralized">decentralized</a> management and separate divisions for each price class.

--- Chunk 706 ---
They introduced annual model changes.

--- Chunk 707 ---
GM became an innovator in technology under the leadership of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_F._Kettering" title="Charles F. Kettering">Charles F. Kettering</a>.

--- Chunk 708 ---
GM followed Ford by expanding overseas, including purchasing England's <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vauxhall_Motors" title="Vauxhall Motors">Vauxhall Motors</a> in 1925, Germany's <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Opel" title="Opel">Opel</a> in 1929, and Australia's <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Holden" title="Holden">Holden</a> in 1931.

--- Chunk 709 ---
GM established GMAC, now <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ally_Financial" title="Ally Financial">Ally Financial</a>, in 1919 to provide credit for buyers of its cars.

--- Chunk 710 ---
<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Walter_Chrysler" title="Walter Chrysler">Walter Chrysler</a> was formerly president of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Buick" title="Buick">Buick</a> and an executive of GM.

--- Chunk 711 ---
After leaving GM in 1920, he took control of the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maxwell_(automobile)" class="mw-redirect" title="Maxwell (automobile)">Maxwell Motor Company</a>, revitalized the company and, in 1925, reorganized it into <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chrysler" title="Chrysler">Chrysler Corporation</a>.

--- Chunk 712 ---
In 1927, he acquired <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dodge" title="Dodge">Dodge</a>.

--- Chunk 713 ---
The acquisition of Dodge gave Chrysler the manufacturing facilities and dealer network that it needed to significantly expand production and sales.

--- Chunk 714 ---
In 1928, Chrysler introduced the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plymouth_(automobile)" title="Plymouth (automobile)">Plymouth</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DeSoto_(automobile)" title="DeSoto (automobile)">DeSoto</a> brands.

--- Chunk 715 ---
Chrysler overtook Ford to become the second largest auto maker by the 1930s, following similar strategies as General Motors.

--- Chunk 716 ---
General Motors wanted automobiles to be not just utilitarian devices, which Ford emphasized, but status symbols that were highly visible indicators of an individual's wealth.

--- Chunk 717 ---
Through offering different makes and models, they offered different levels in social status, meeting the demands of consumers needing to display wealth.

--- Chunk 718 ---
Ford and General Motors each had their own impact on social status and the type of market they were targeting.

--- Chunk 719 ---
Henry Ford focused on delivering one inexpensive, efficient product for the masses.

--- Chunk 720 ---
Ford's offer was one car, one color, for one price.

--- Chunk 721 ---
He manufactured a product for the masses, and provided a $5 daily wage so that there was a local market to buy this product.

--- Chunk 722 ---
By contrast, General Motors offered a product that catered to those looking to gain status by having that sense of individualism and offering different make, models, and quality.

--- Chunk 723 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>

--- Chunk 724 ---
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2">

--- Chunk 725 ---
<h2 id="Great_Depression_and_World_War_II">

--- Chunk 726 ---
Great Depression and World War II

--- Chunk 727 ---
<span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Great Depression and World War II"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>

--- Chunk 728 ---
The 1930s saw the demise of many auto makers due to the economic effects of the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>, stiff competition from the Big Three, and/or mismanagement.

--- Chunk 729 ---
Luxury car makers were particularly affected by the economy, with companies like <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stutz_Motor_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="Stutz Motor Company">Stutz Motor Company</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pierce-Arrow_Motor_Car_Company" title="Pierce-Arrow Motor Car Company">Pierce-Arrow Motor Car Company</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peerless_Motor_Company" title="Peerless Motor Company">Peerless Motor Company</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cunningham_automobile" class="mw-redirect" title="Cunningham automobile">Cunningham</a>, and the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marmon_Motor_Car_Company" title="Marmon Motor Car Company">Marmon Motor Car Company</a> going out of business.

--- Chunk 730 ---
The 1930s also saw several companies with innovative engineering, such as the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doble_steam_car" title="Doble steam car">Doble Steam Motors</a> Corporation (advanced steam engines) and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Franklin_(automobile)" title="Franklin (automobile)">Franklin Automobile Company</a> (air-cooled aluminium engines) going out of business.

--- Chunk 731 ---
<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Errett_Lobban_Cord" title="Errett Lobban Cord">Errett Lobban Cord</a>, who controlled the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Auburn_Automobile" title="Auburn Automobile">Auburn Automobile</a> Company (which also sold the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cord_(automobile)" title="Cord (automobile)">Cord</a>) and the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Duesenberg" title="Duesenberg">Duesenberg</a> Motor Company, was under investigation by the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Securities_and_Exchange_Commission" class="mw-redirect" title="Securities and Exchange Commission">Securities and Exchange Commission</a> and the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internal_Revenue_Service" title="Internal Revenue Service">Internal Revenue Service</a>.

--- Chunk 732 ---
His auto empire collapsed in 1937 and production ceased.

--- Chunk 733 ---
Major technological innovations were introduced or were widely adopted during the 1930s, such as synchromesh manual transmissions, semi-automatic transmissions, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Automatic_transmission" title="Automatic transmission">automatic transmissions</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydraulic_brake" title="Hydraulic brake">hydraulic brakes</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Independent_front_suspension" class="mw-redirect" title="Independent front suspension">independent front suspension</a>, and overhead-valve engines.

--- Chunk 734 ---
The Cord 810 used front-wheel drive, had hidden headlights, and was offered with a supercharger.

--- Chunk 735 ---
Exterior styling designs were more flowing, as shown most noticeably on the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Auburn_Automobile" title="Auburn Automobile">Auburn</a> Speedster and the Cord 810/812.

--- Chunk 736 ---
Radical air-streamed design was introduced on the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chrysler_Airflow" title="Chrysler Airflow">Chrysler Airflow</a>, a sales flop, and the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lincoln-Zephyr" title="Lincoln-Zephyr">Lincoln-Zephyr</a> (both of which used unit-body construction).

--- Chunk 737 ---
<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Packard" title="Packard">Packard</a> introduced their "Air Cool-ditioned" car in 1940.

--- Chunk 738 ---
After the United States entered <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> in December 1941, all auto plants were converted to war production, including jeeps, trucks, tanks, and aircraft engines.

--- Chunk 739 ---
All passenger automobile production ceased by February 1942.

--- Chunk 740 ---
The industry received $10 billion in war-related orders by that month, compared to $4 billion before the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Attack_on_Pearl_Harbor" title="Attack on Pearl Harbor">attack on Pearl Harbor</a>.

--- Chunk 741 ---
All factories were enlarged and converted, many new ones such as Ford's <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Willow_Run" title="Willow Run">Willow Run</a> and Chrysler's <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Detroit_Arsenal_(Warren,_Michigan)" title="Detroit Arsenal (Warren, Michigan)">Detroit Arsenal Tank Plant</a> were built, and hundreds of thousands more workers were hired.

--- Chunk 742 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-life19420216_31-0" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-life19420216-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>

--- Chunk 743 ---
Many workers were new arrivals from Appalachia.

--- Chunk 744 ---
The most distinctive new product was the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jeep" title="Jeep">Jeep</a>, with Willys making 352,000 and Ford another 295,000.

--- Chunk 745 ---
The industry produced an astonishing amount of material, including 5.9 million weapons, 2.8 million tanks and trucks, and 27,000 aircraft.

--- Chunk 746 ---
This production was a major factor in the victory of the allies.

--- Chunk 747 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Experts anticipated that Detroit would learn advanced engineering methods from the aviation industry that would result in great improvements for postwar civilian automobiles.

--- Chunk 748 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-life19420216_31-1" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-life19420216-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>

--- Chunk 749 ---
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2">

--- Chunk 750 ---
<h2 id="Unionization_of_the_auto_manufacturers_workforce">

--- Chunk 751 ---
Unionization of the auto manufacturers workforce

--- Chunk 752 ---
<span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Unionization of the auto manufacturers workforce"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>

--- Chunk 753 ---
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" />

--- Chunk 754 ---
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">

--- Chunk 755 ---
See also: <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Automobile_Workers" class="mw-redirect" title="United Automobile Workers">United Automobile Workers</a>

--- Chunk 756 ---
Due to the difficult working conditions in the auto production plants, auto workers began to seek representation to help improve conditions and ensure fair pay.

--- Chunk 757 ---
The <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Automobile_Workers" class="mw-redirect" title="United Automobile Workers">United Automobile Workers</a> union won recognition from GM and Chrysler in 1937, and Ford in 1941.

--- Chunk 758 ---
In 1950, the automakers granted workers a company-paid pension to those 65 years old and with 30 years seniority.

--- Chunk 759 ---
In the mid-1950s, the automakers agreed to set up a trust fund for unemployed auto workers.

--- Chunk 760 ---
In 1973, the automakers agreed to offer pensions to any worker with 30 years seniority, regardless of age.

--- Chunk 761 ---
By then the automakers had also agreed to cover the entire health insurance bill for its employees, survivors, and retirees.

--- Chunk 762 ---
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2">

--- Chunk 763 ---
<h2 id="Decline_of_the_independent_automakers">

--- Chunk 764 ---
Decline of the independent automakers

--- Chunk 765 ---
<span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Decline of the independent automakers"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>

--- Chunk 766 ---
The only major auto companies to survive the Great Depression were <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors Corporation</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company" title="Ford Motor Company">Ford Motor Company</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chrysler" title="Chrysler">Chrysler Corporation</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hudson_Motor_Car_Company" title="Hudson Motor Car Company">Hudson Motor Car Company</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nash-Kelvinator" title="Nash-Kelvinator">Nash-Kelvinator Corporation</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Packard" title="Packard">Packard Motor Car Company</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Studebaker" title="Studebaker">Studebaker Corporation</a>, and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crosley" title="Crosley">Crosley Motors</a>.

--- Chunk 767 ---
The former three companies, known as the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_Three_(automobile_manufacturers)" title="Big Three (automobile manufacturers)">Big Three</a>, enjoyed significant advantages over the smaller independent auto companies due to their financial strength, which gave them a big edge in marketing, production, and technological innovation.

--- Chunk 768 ---
Most of the Big Three's competitors ended production by the 1960s, and their last major domestic competitor- American Motors Corporation, was acquired in 1987.

--- Chunk 769 ---
Crosley Motors ceased auto production in 1952.

--- Chunk 770 ---
Packard and Studebaker merged in 1954, but ended production of Packard-branded cars in 1958 and ceased all auto production in 1966.

--- Chunk 771 ---
<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kaiser-Frazer" title="Kaiser-Frazer">Kaiser-Frazer Corporation</a> was started in 1945 and acquired <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Willys" title="Willys">Willys-Overland Motors</a> (maker of the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jeep" title="Jeep">Jeep</a>) in 1953.

--- Chunk 772 ---
Production of passenger cars was discontinued in 1955.

--- Chunk 773 ---
In 1970, the company was sold to <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Motors_Corporation" title="American Motors Corporation">American Motors Corporation</a>.

--- Chunk 774 ---
In 1954, Nash-Kelvinator and Hudson merged to form <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Motors_Corporation" title="American Motors Corporation">American Motors Corporation</a> (AMC).

--- Chunk 775 ---
The company introduced numerous product and marketing innovations, but its small size made it difficult to compete with the Big Three and struggled financially.

--- Chunk 776 ---
The French auto maker <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Renault" title="Renault">Renault</a> took control of AMC in the early 1980s, but financial difficulties continued and AMC was purchased by Chrysler Corporation in 1987.

--- Chunk 777 ---
Periodically, other entrepreneurs would found automobile companies, but most would soon fail and none achieved major sales success.

--- Chunk 778 ---
Some of the best known included <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Preston_Tucker" title="Preston Tucker">Preston Tucker</a>'s <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tucker_48" title="Tucker 48">1948 sedan</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Madman_Muntz" title="Madman Muntz">Earl Muntz</a>'s <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muntz_Car_Company" title="Muntz Car Company">Muntz Car Company</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Malcolm_Bricklin" title="Malcolm Bricklin">Malcolm Bricklin</a>'s <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bricklin_SV-1" title="Bricklin SV-1">Bricklin SV-1</a>, the modern <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stutz_Blackhawk" title="Stutz Blackhawk">Stutz Blackhawk</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cl%C3%A9net_Coachworks" title="Clénet Coachworks">Clénet Coachworks</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zimmer_(automobile)" title="Zimmer (automobile)">Zimmer</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Excalibur_(automobile)" title="Excalibur (automobile)">Excalibur</a>, and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_DeLorean" title="John DeLorean">John DeLorean</a>'s <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DMC_DeLorean" title="DMC DeLorean">DeLorean</a>.

--- Chunk 779 ---
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2">

--- Chunk 780 ---
<h2 id="Post-war_years">

--- Chunk 781 ---
<span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Post-war years"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>

--- Chunk 782 ---
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" />

--- Chunk 783 ---
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">

--- Chunk 784 ---
See also: <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_automobile_industry_in_the_1950s" title="American automobile industry in the 1950s">American automobile industry in the 1950s</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1950s_American_automobile_culture" title="1950s American automobile culture">1950s American automobile culture</a>

--- Chunk 785 ---
Initial auto production after World War II was slowed by the retooling process, shortages of materials, and labor unrest.

--- Chunk 786 ---
However, the American auto industry reflected the post-war prosperity of the late-1940s and the 1950s.

--- Chunk 787 ---
Cars grew in overall size, as well as engine size during the 1950s.

--- Chunk 788 ---
The <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Overhead_valve_engine" title="Overhead valve engine">Overhead valve</a> V-8 engine developed by GM in the late-1940s proved to be very successful and helped ignite the horsepower race, the second salvo of which was Chrysler's 1951 <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hemispherical_combustion_chamber" title="Hemispherical combustion chamber">Hemi engine</a>.

--- Chunk 789 ---
Longer, lower, and wider tended to be the general trend.

--- Chunk 790 ---
Exterior styling was influenced by jets and rockets as the space-age dawned.

--- Chunk 791 ---
Rear fins were popular and continued to grow larger, and front bumpers and taillights were sometimes designed in the shape of rockets.

--- Chunk 792 ---
Chrome plating was very popular, as was two-tone paint.

--- Chunk 793 ---
The most extreme version of these styling trends were found in the 1959 <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cadillac_Eldorado" title="Cadillac Eldorado">Cadillac Eldorado</a> and Chrysler Corporation's 1957 <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Imperial_(automobile)" title="Imperial (automobile)">Imperial</a>.

--- Chunk 794 ---
The <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chevrolet_Corvette" title="Chevrolet Corvette">Chevrolet Corvette</a> and the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ford_Thunderbird" title="Ford Thunderbird">Ford Thunderbird</a>, introduced in 1953 and 1955 respectively, were designed to capture the sports car market.

--- Chunk 795 ---
The Thunderbird grew in size in 1958 and evolved into a <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Personal_luxury_car" title="Personal luxury car">personal luxury car</a>.

--- Chunk 796 ---
The 1950s were also noted for perhaps one of the biggest miscues in auto marketing with the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edsel" title="Edsel">Ford Edsel</a>, which was the result of unpopular styling and being introduced during an economic recession.

--- Chunk 797 ---
The introduction of the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Interstate_Highway_System" title="Interstate Highway System">Interstate Highway System</a><sup id="cite_ref-Genie_33-0" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-Genie-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and the suburbanization of America made automobiles more necessary<sup id="cite_ref-Magazines1958_34-0" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-Magazines1958-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and helped change the landscape and culture in the United States.

--- Chunk 798 ---
Individuals began to see the automobile as an extension of themselves.

--- Chunk 799 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-hofstra_35-0" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-hofstra-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>

--- Chunk 800 ---
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2">

--- Chunk 801 ---
<span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: 1960s"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>

--- Chunk 802 ---
Big changes were taking place in automobile development in the 1960s, with the Big Three dominating the industry.

--- Chunk 803 ---
Meanwhile, with the passage of the $33 billion <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Federal_Aid_Highway_Act_of_1956" class="mw-redirect" title="Federal Aid Highway Act of 1956">Federal Aid Highway Act of 1956</a>, a network of regional and interstate roads continued to enhance transportation.

--- Chunk 804 ---
As urban areas became more congested, more families migrated to the suburbs.

--- Chunk 805 ---
Between 1960 and 1970, 70 percent of the population's growth occurred in the suburbs.

--- Chunk 806 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>

--- Chunk 807 ---
Imported vehicles grew during the 1950s and 1960s – from a very low base.

--- Chunk 808 ---
In 1966, the <i>Big Three</i> (GM, Ford, Chrysler) had market share of 89.6% (44.5% in 2014).

--- Chunk 809 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> From 1966 to 1969, net imports increased at an average annual rate of 84%.

--- Chunk 810 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volkswagen_Beetle" title="Volkswagen Beetle">Volkswagen Beetle</a> was the biggest seller.

--- Chunk 811 ---
The compact <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nash_Rambler" title="Nash Rambler">Nash Rambler</a> had been around since 1950, and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Motors_Corporation" title="American Motors Corporation">American Motors Corporation</a> (AMC) expanded into a range of smaller cars than were offered by the Big Three.

--- Chunk 812 ---
By 1960, Rambler was the third most popular brand of automobile in the United States, behind Ford and Chevrolet.

--- Chunk 813 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In response to this the domestic auto makers developed compact-sized cars, such as the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ford_Falcon_(North_America)" title="Ford Falcon (North America)">Ford Falcon</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chevrolet_Corvair" title="Chevrolet Corvair">Chevrolet Corvair</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Studebaker_Lark" title="Studebaker Lark">Studebaker Lark</a>, and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plymouth_Valiant" title="Plymouth Valiant">Plymouth Valiant</a>.

--- Chunk 814 ---
The four-seat 1958 <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ford_Thunderbird_(second_generation)" title="Ford Thunderbird (second generation)">Ford Thunderbird (second generation)</a> was arguably the first <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Personal_luxury_car" title="Personal luxury car">personal luxury car</a>, which became a large market segment.

--- Chunk 815 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-Genat_40-0" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-Genat-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>

--- Chunk 816 ---
<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pony_car" title="Pony car">Pony cars</a> were introduced with the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ford_Mustang" title="Ford Mustang">Ford Mustang</a> in 1964.

--- Chunk 817 ---
This car combined sporty looks with a long hood, small rear deck, and a small rear seat.

--- Chunk 818 ---
The car proved highly successful and imitators soon arose, including the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chevrolet_Camaro" title="Chevrolet Camaro">Chevrolet Camaro</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pontiac_Firebird" title="Pontiac Firebird">Pontiac Firebird</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plymouth_Barracuda" title="Plymouth Barracuda">Plymouth Barracuda</a> (actually introduced two weeks prior to the Mustang), <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AMC_Javelin" title="AMC Javelin">AMC Javelin</a>, and the two-seat <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AMC_AMX" title="AMC AMX">AMX</a>, as well as the "luxury" version of the Mustang, the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mercury_Cougar" title="Mercury Cougar">Mercury Cougar</a>.

--- Chunk 819 ---
<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muscle_car" title="Muscle car">Muscle cars</a> were introduced in 1964 with the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pontiac_GTO" title="Pontiac GTO">Pontiac GTO</a>.

--- Chunk 820 ---
These combined an intermediate-sized body with a large high-output engine.

--- Chunk 821 ---
Competitors were quickly introduced, including the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chevrolet_Chevelle_SS" class="mw-redirect" title="Chevrolet Chevelle SS">Chevrolet Chevelle SS</a>, Dodge R/T (<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dodge_Coronet" title="Dodge Coronet">Coronet</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dodge_Charger" title="Dodge Charger">Charger</a>), <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plymouth_Road_Runner" title="Plymouth Road Runner">Plymouth Road Runner</a>/GTX, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ford_Torino" title="Ford Torino">Ford Torino</a>, and AMC's compact <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rambler_American#SC/Rambler" title="Rambler American">SC/Rambler</a>.

--- Chunk 822 ---
Muscle cars reached their peak in the late-1960s, but soon fell out of favor due to high insurance premiums along with the combination of emission controls and high gas prices in the early 1970s.

--- Chunk 823 ---
While the personal luxury, pony, and muscle cars got most of the attention, the full sized cars formed the bulk of auto sales in the 1960s, helped by low oil prices.

--- Chunk 824 ---
The styling excesses and technological gimmicks (such as the retractable hardtop and the pushbutton automatic transmission) of the 1950s were de-emphasized.

--- Chunk 825 ---
The rear fins were downsized and largely gone by the mid-1960s, as was the excessive chrome.

--- Chunk 826 ---
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2">

--- Chunk 827 ---
<h2 id="Federal_regulation_of_the_auto_industry">

--- Chunk 828 ---
Federal regulation of the auto industry

--- Chunk 829 ---
<span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Federal regulation of the auto industry"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>

--- Chunk 830 ---
Safety and environmental issues during the 1960s led to stricter government regulation of the auto industry, spurred in part by <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ralph_Nader" title="Ralph Nader">Ralph Nader</a> and his book: <i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unsafe_at_Any_Speed" class="mw-redirect" title="Unsafe at Any Speed">Unsafe at Any Speed</a>: The Designed-in Dangers of the American Automobile</i>.

--- Chunk 831 ---
This resulted in higher costs and eventually to weaker performance for cars in the 1970s, a period known as the <i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Malaise_era" title="Malaise era">Malaise Era</a></i> of auto design <sup id="cite_ref-roadkill_41-0" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-roadkill-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> during which American cars suffered from very poor performance.

--- Chunk 832 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-popmech_42-0" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-popmech-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>

--- Chunk 833 ---
Seat lap belts were mandated by many states effective in 1962.

--- Chunk 834 ---
Under the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Traffic_and_Motor_Vehicle_Safety_Act" title="National Traffic and Motor Vehicle Safety Act">National Traffic and Motor Vehicle Safety Act</a> of 1966, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Federal_Motor_Vehicle_Safety_Standards" title="Federal Motor Vehicle Safety Standards">Federal Motor Vehicle Safety Standards</a> required shoulder belts for front passengers, front head restraints, energy-absorbing steering columns, ignition-key warning systems, anti-theft steering column/transmission locks, side marker lights and padded interiors starting in 1968.

--- Chunk 835 ---
Beginning in 1972, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bumper_(automobile)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bumper (automobile)">bumpers</a> were required to be reinforced to meet 5-mph impact standards, a decision that was revised in 1982.

--- Chunk 836 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-Billion_43-0" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-Billion-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>

--- Chunk 837 ---
With the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clean_Air_Act_(United_States)" title="Clean Air Act (United States)">Clean Air Act</a> of 1963 and the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Emissions_Standards_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="National Emissions Standards Act">Vehicle Air Pollution and Control Act</a> of 1965, emission controls began being instituted in 1968.

--- Chunk 838 ---
The use of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leaded_gasoline" class="mw-redirect" title="Leaded gasoline">leaded gasoline</a> began being curtailed in the early 1970s, which resulted in lower-compression engines being used, and thus reducing horsepower and performance.

--- Chunk 839 ---
<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Catalytic_converter" title="Catalytic converter">Catalytic converters</a> began being widely used by the mid-1970s.

--- Chunk 840 ---
During his first term as EPA Administrator, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Ruckelshaus" title="William Ruckelshaus">William Ruckelshaus</a> spent 60% of his time on the automobile industry, whose emissions were to be reduced 90% under the 1970 Clean Air Act after senators became frustrated at the industry's failure to cut emissions under previous, weaker air laws.

--- Chunk 841 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>

--- Chunk 842 ---
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2">

--- Chunk 843 ---
<span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: 1970s"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>

--- Chunk 844 ---
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" />

--- Chunk 845 ---
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">

--- Chunk 846 ---
See also: <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Malaise_era" title="Malaise era">Malaise era</a>

--- Chunk 847 ---
As bold and confident as the Big Three automakers were in the 1950s and 1960s, the American auto makers in the 1970s and 1980s stumbled badly, going from one engineering, manufacturing, or marketing disaster to another, and this time is often referred to as the <i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Malaise_era" title="Malaise era">Malaise era</a></i> of American auto design.

--- Chunk 848 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-popmech_42-1" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-popmech-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>

--- Chunk 849 ---
By 1969, imports had increased their share of the U.

--- Chunk 850 ---
S.  auto market with smaller, inexpensive vehicles.

--- Chunk 851 ---
<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volkswagen" title="Volkswagen">Volkswagen</a> sold over 500,000 vehicles, followed by <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Toyota" title="Toyota">Toyota</a> with over 100,000.

--- Chunk 852 ---
In 1986 South Korea entered the American market.

--- Chunk 853 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In response to this, the domestic auto makers introduced new compact and sub-compact cars, such as the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ford_Pinto" title="Ford Pinto">Ford Pinto</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ford_Maverick_(Americas)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ford Maverick (Americas)">Maverick</a>, the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chevrolet_Vega" title="Chevrolet Vega">Chevrolet Vega</a>, and the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AMC_Gremlin" title="AMC Gremlin">AMC Gremlin</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AMC_Hornet" title="AMC Hornet">Hornet</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AMC_Pacer" title="AMC Pacer">Pacer</a>.  (Chrysler had to make do with importing the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dodge_Colt" title="Dodge Colt">Dodge Colt</a> from <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mitsubishi_Motors" title="Mitsubishi Motors">Mitsubishi Motors</a> and the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hillman_Avenger" title="Hillman Avenger">Plymouth Cricket</a> from their affiliated <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rootes_Group" title="Rootes Group">Rootes Group</a>.) However, design and manufacturing problems plagued a number of these cars, leading to unfavorable consumer perceptions.

--- Chunk 854 ---
GM had a string of miscues starting with the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chevrolet_Vega" title="Chevrolet Vega">Chevrolet Vega</a>, which developed a reputation for rapidly rusting and having major problems with the aluminium engine.

--- Chunk 855 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>

--- Chunk 856 ---
The problems with Ford's Pinto became nationally famous and Ford's reputation was harmed after media accusations that its <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ford_Pinto#Fuel_system_fires,_recalls,_and_litigation" title="Ford Pinto">fuel system was prone to fire</a> when the car was struck from behind.

--- Chunk 857 ---
It was also alleged that Ford knew about this vulnerability but did not design any safeguards in order to save a few dollars per vehicle and that the company rationalized that the cost of lawsuits would be less than the cost of redesigning the car.

--- Chunk 858 ---
Historical analysis of the facts do not support the "death trap" reputation attached to the Pinto but the damage to Ford's reputation had been done.

--- Chunk 859 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>

--- Chunk 860 ---
Auto sales were hurt by the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1973_oil_crisis" title="1973 oil crisis">1973 oil crisis</a> Arab embargo as the price of gasoline soared.

--- Chunk 861 ---
Small fuel-efficient cars from foreign automakers took a sharply higher share of the U.

--- Chunk 862 ---
S.  auto sales market.

--- Chunk 863 ---
Under the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Energy_Policy_and_Conservation_Act" title="Energy Policy and Conservation Act">Energy Policy and Conservation Act</a><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the federal government initiated fuel efficiency standards (known as <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Corporate_Average_Fuel_Economy" class="mw-redirect" title="Corporate Average Fuel Economy">Corporate Average Fuel Economy</a>, or CAFE) in 1975, effective as of 1978 for passenger cars, and as of 1979 for light trucks.

--- Chunk 864 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
For passenger cars, the initial standard was 18 miles per gallon (mpg), and increased to 27.5 mpg by 1985.

--- Chunk 865 ---
General Motors began responding first to the high gas prices by <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Downsize_(automobile)" title="Downsize (automobile)">downsizing</a> most of their models by 1977, and lowering their performance.

--- Chunk 866 ---
In 1979, the second oil price spike occurred, precipitated by political events in Iran, resulting in the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1979_energy_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="1979 energy crisis">1979 energy crisis</a>.

--- Chunk 867 ---
By 1980, the economy slid into turmoil, with high inflation, high unemployment, and high interest rates.

--- Chunk 868 ---
The automakers suffered large operating losses.

--- Chunk 869 ---
Chrysler was hurt most severely and in 1979 received a bailout from the federal government in the form of $1.5 billion in loan guarantees.

--- Chunk 870 ---
One quick fix was a Detroit-built version of their then-new French (<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Simca" title="Simca">Simca</a>) economy car, the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chrysler_Horizon" title="Chrysler Horizon">Horizon</a>.

--- Chunk 871 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As a result of its financial difficulties, Chrysler sold its British and French subsidiaries, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rootes_Group" title="Rootes Group">Rootes Group</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Simca" title="Simca">Simca</a> to the French automaker <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Groupe_PSA" class="mw-redirect" title="Groupe PSA">Groupe PSA</a> for $1.

--- Chunk 872 ---
Cadillac damaged their reputation when the four-cylinder <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cadillac_Cimarron" title="Cadillac Cimarron">Cadillac Cimarron</a> was introduced in 1981 (a gussied-up <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chevrolet_Cavalier" title="Chevrolet Cavalier">Chevrolet Cavalier</a> at twice the price) and the "V8-6-4" engine did not work as advertised.

--- Chunk 873 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> GM's reputation was also damaged when it revealed in 1977 that they were installing <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chevrolet" title="Chevrolet">Chevrolet</a> engines in <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oldsmobile" title="Oldsmobile">Oldsmobiles</a>, and lawsuits from aggrieved Oldsmobile owners followed.

--- Chunk 874 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Likewise litigation ensued when a trio of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diesel_engine" title="Diesel engine">diesel engines</a>, designed from gasoline engines and used in GM cars from 1978 to 1985 suffered major problems.

--- Chunk 875 ---
Class action lawsuits and efforts from the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Federal_Trade_Commission" title="Federal Trade Commission">Federal Trade Commission</a> resulted in buybacks of the cars from GM.

--- Chunk 876 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Chrysler also suffered damage to its reputation when its compact cars, the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plymouth_Volar%C3%A9" class="mw-redirect" title="Plymouth Volaré">Plymouth Volaré</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dodge_Aspen" title="Dodge Aspen">Dodge Aspen</a>, were developed quickly and suffered from massive recalls and poor quality.

--- Chunk 877 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>

--- Chunk 878 ---
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2">

--- Chunk 879 ---
<span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: 1980s"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>

--- Chunk 880 ---
In 1981, Japanese automakers entered into the "<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Voluntary_export_restraint" title="Voluntary export restraint">voluntary export restraint</a>" limiting the number of autos that they could export to the U.

--- Chunk 881 ---
S.  to 1.68 million per year.

--- Chunk 882 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> One side effect of this quota was that Japanese car companies opened new divisions through which they began developing luxury cars that had higher profit margins, such as with <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Toyota" title="Toyota">Toyota</a>'s <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lexus" title="Lexus">Lexus</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Honda" title="Honda">Honda</a>'s <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acura" title="Acura">Acura</a>, and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nissan" title="Nissan">Nissan</a>'s <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infiniti" title="Infiniti">Infiniti</a>.

--- Chunk 883 ---
Another consequence was that the Japanese car makers began opening auto production plants in the U.

--- Chunk 884 ---
The three largest Japanese auto manufacturers all opened production facilities by 1985.

--- Chunk 885 ---
These facilities were opened primarily in the southern states because of financial incentives offered by state governments, access to the nation via the interstate highways, the availability of a large pool of cheaper labor, and the weakness of unions.

--- Chunk 886 ---
The Southern states passed <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Right-to-work_law" title="Right-to-work law">right-to-work laws</a> and the UAW failed in its repeated union-organizing efforts at these plants.

--- Chunk 887 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>

--- Chunk 888 ---
The Big Three began investing in and/or developing joint manufacturing facilities with several of the Japanese automakers.

--- Chunk 889 ---
Ford invested in <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mazda" title="Mazda">Mazda</a> as well as setting up a joint facility with them called <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AutoAlliance_International" class="mw-redirect" title="AutoAlliance International">AutoAlliance International</a>.

--- Chunk 890 ---
Chrysler bought stock in <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mitsubishi_Motors" title="Mitsubishi Motors">Mitsubishi Motors</a> and established a joint facility with them called <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diamond-Star_Motors" title="Diamond-Star Motors">Diamond-Star Motors</a>.

--- Chunk 891 ---
GM invested in <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suzuki" title="Suzuki">Suzuki</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isuzu" title="Isuzu">Isuzu</a>, and set up a joint manufacturing facility with <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Toyota" title="Toyota">Toyota</a>, called <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NUMMI" title="NUMMI">NUMMI</a> (New United Motor Manufacturing, Inc.).

--- Chunk 892 ---
<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States.html#cite_note-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>

--- Chunk 893 ---
Despite the financial and marketing upheavals during the 1970s and 1980s, these decades led to technological innovations and/or widespread use of such improvements as <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Disc_brake" title="Disc brake">disc brakes</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fuel_injection" title="Fuel injection">fuel injection</a>, electronic <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Engine_control_unit" title="Engine control unit">engine control units</a>, and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electronic_ignition" class="mw-redirect" title="Electronic ignition">electronic ignition</a>.

--- Chunk 894 ---
<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Front-wheel_drive" title="Front-wheel drive">Front-wheel drive</a> became the standard drive system by the late 1980s.

--- Chunk 895 ---
By the mid-1980s, oil prices had fallen sharply, helping lead to the revitalization of the American auto industry.

--- Chunk 896 ---
Under the leadership of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lee_Iacocca" title="Lee Iacocca">Lee Iacocca</a>, Chrysler Corporation mounted a comeback after its flirtation with bankruptcy in 1979.

--- Chunk 897 ---
The <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Minivan" title="Minivan">minivan</a> was introduced in the 1984 model year by Chrysler with the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plymouth_Voyager" title="Plymouth Voyager">Plymouth Voyager</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dodge_Caravan" title="Dodge Caravan">Dodge Caravan</a>, and proved very popular.

--- Chunk 898 ---
These vehicles were built on a passenger-car chassis and seated up to seven people as well as being able to hold bulky loads.

--- Chunk 899 ---
Chrysler introduced their "<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chrysler_K_platform" title="Chrysler K platform">K-cars</a>" in the 1980s, which came with front-wheel drive and fuel-efficient OHC engines.

--- Chunk 900 ---
In 1987, Chrysler bought <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Motors_Corporation" title="American Motors Corporation">American Motors Corporation</a>, which produced the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jeep" title="Jeep">Jeep</a>.

--- Chunk 901 ---
This proved to be excellent timing to take advantage of the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sport_utility_vehicle" class="mw-redirect" title="Sport utility vehicle">sport utility vehicle</a> boom.

--- Chunk 902 ---
Ford began a comeback after losses of $3.3 billion in the early 1980s.

--- Chunk 903 ---
In 1985, the company introduced the very successful, aerodynamic <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ford_Taurus" title="Ford Taurus">Taurus</a>.

--- Chunk 904 ---
General Motors, under the leadership of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roger_Smith_(executive)" title="Roger Smith (executive)">Roger Smith</a>, was not as successful as its competitors in turning itself around, and its market share fell significantly.

--- Chunk 905 ---
While Ford and Chrysler were cutting production costs, GM was investing heavily in new technology.

--- Chunk 906 ---
The company's attempts at overhauling its management structure and using increased technology for manufacturing production were not successful.

--- Chunk 907 ---
Several large acquisitions (<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electronic_Data_Systems" title="Electronic Data Systems">Electronic Data Systems</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hughes_Aircraft_Company" title="Hughes Aircraft Company">Hughes Aircraft Company</a>) diverted management attention away from their main industry.

--- Chunk 908 ---
Ford and Chrysler joined in the acquisition and diversification trend, with Ford buying <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jaguar_Cars" title="Jaguar Cars">Jaguar Cars</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aston_Martin" title="Aston Martin">Aston Martin</a>, The Associates (a finance company), and First Nationwide Financial Corp, a savings and loan company.

--- Chunk 909 ---
Chrysler purchased <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lamborghini" title="Lamborghini">Lamborghini</a>, an interest in <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maserati" title="Maserati">Maserati</a>, and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gulfstream_Aerospace" title="Gulfstream Aerospace">Gulfstream Aerospace</a> jets.

--- Chunk 910 ---
GM started the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saturn_Corporation" title="Saturn Corporation">Saturn</a> brand in the late 1980s as a way to retake sales from imported cars.

--- Chunk 911 ---
While Saturn initially succeeded, GM later neglected to provide it much support.

--- Chunk 912 ---
Around this time GM began development on the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/General_Motors_EV1" title="General Motors EV1">General Motors EV1</a> electric car, which debuted in 1996.

--- Chunk 913 ---
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2">

--- Chunk 914 ---
<span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: 1990s"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>

--- Chunk 915 ---
The 1990s began the decade in a recession, which resulted in weak auto sales and operating losses.

--- Chunk 916 ---
The <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Invasion_of_Kuwait" class="mw-redirect" title="Invasion of Kuwait">Invasion of Kuwait</a> by <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> caused a temporary jump in oil prices.

--- Chunk 917 ---
The automakers recovered fairly quickly.

--- Chunk 918 ---
In the mid-1990s, light truck sales, which included <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sport_utility_vehicle" class="mw-redirect" title="Sport utility vehicle">sport utility vehicles</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pickup_truck" title="Pickup truck">pickup trucks</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Minivan" title="Minivan">minivans</a>, began to rise sharply.

--- Chunk 919 ---
Due to the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Corporate_Average_Fuel_Economy" class="mw-redirect" title="Corporate Average Fuel Economy">Corporate Average Fuel Economy</a> standards differentiating between passenger cars and light trucks, the automakers were able to sell large and heavy vehicles without fear of the CAFE fines.

--- Chunk 920 ---
Low oil prices gave incentives for consumers to buy these gas-guzzling vehicles.

--- Chunk 921 ---
The American automakers sold combined, and even separately, millions of pickup trucks and body-on-frame SUVs during this period.

--- Chunk 922 ---
Imports such as the Toyota 4Runner, Land Cruiser, Tacoma, and Nissan Pathfinder and Frontier were also popular during this time period.

--- Chunk 923 ---
The automakers also continued their trend of purchasing or investing in foreign automakers.

--- Chunk 924 ---
